https:\u002F\u002Fvideo-images.vice.com\u002F_uncategorized\u002F1542036107018-Screen-Shot-2018-11-12-at-95713-AM.jpeg?resize=1575:* 2x\"\u003E \u003Csource media=\"(min-width: 65.625em)\" srcset=\"data:image\u002Fgif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAD\u002FACwAAAAAAQABAAACADs=\" data-srcset=\"https:\u002F\u002Fvideo-images.vice.com\u002F_uncategorized\u002F1542036107018-Screen-Shot-2018-11-12-at-95713-AM.jpeg?resize=1050:*, https:\u002F\u002Fvideo-images.vice.com\u002F_uncategorized\u002F1542036107018-Screen-Shot-2018-11-12-at-95713-AM.jpeg?resize=1575:* 2x\"\u003E \u003Cimg src=\"data:image\u002Fgif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAD\u002FACwAAAAAAQABAAACADs=\" alt=\"1542036107018-Screen-Shot-2018-11-12-at-95713-AM\" class=\"col-12-xs\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fvideo-images.vice.com\u002F_uncategorized\u002F1542036107018-Screen-Shot-2018-11-12-at-95713-AM.jpeg\"\u003E \u003C\u002Fpicture\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"article__image-caption\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E \u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E \u003Cp\u003EIn the wake of September 11th, the United States built out a massive surveillance apparatus, undermined \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.aclu.org\u002Finfographic\u002Fsurveillance-under-patriot-act\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E constitutional protections\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E, and limited possible \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fen.wikipedia.org\u002Fwiki\u002FAshcroft_v._Iqbal\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E recourse to the legal system\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EGiven the extraordinary capabilities of state surveillance in the US—as well as the capabilities of governments around the world such as China and Russia—you might be feeling a little paranoid! It’s not just the NSA—the FBI and even local cops have more tools at their disposal to snoop on people than ever before. And there is a terrifying breadth of passive and unexpected surveillance to worry about: Your social media accounts can be subpoenaed, your emails or calls can be scooped up in bulk collection efforts, and your cell phone metadata can be captured by Stingrays and IMSI catchers meant to target someone else.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"ad-unit__container__container\"\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"article-iac__wrapper\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cp\u003EYou probably aren’t the most at-risk person, but that doesn’t necessarily mean you shouldn’t practice better security. If you’re a privacy-conscious person or believe you may be the target of surveillance, there are some risks you probably don’t want to take that you haven’t even thought about. You probably don’t want to unnecessarily put sensitive data into large databases controlled by corporations, so you shouldn’t, for example, \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fmotherboard.vice.com\u002Fen_us\u002Farticle\u002Fgyma7m\u002Fdont-sequence-your-dna-golden-state-killer\" target=\"_blank\"\u003Eget your DNA sequenced\u003C\u002Fa\u003E by a for-profit company like 23andMe or fill your home with smart devices.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003ESurveillance is a complicated thing: You can practice the best security in the world, but if you’re sending messages to someone who doesn’t, you can still be spied on through their device or through their communications with other people (if they discuss the information you told them, for instance).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EThat’s why it’s important that we normalize good security practices: If you don’t have that much to be afraid of, it’s still important for you to pick up some of these tools, because doing that will normalize the actions of your friends who are, say, undocumented immigrants, or engaged in activism. \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fmotherboard.vice.com\u002Fread\u002Ftrumps-cia-director-pick-thinks-using-encryption-may-itself-be-a-red-flag\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E Trump’s former CIA Director thinks that using encryption “may itself be a red flag.”\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E If you have “nothing to hide,” your use of encryption can actually help people at risk by obfuscating that red flag. By following this guide, you are making someone else safer. Think of it as herd immunity. The more people practice good security, the safer everyone else is.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"ad-unit__container__container\"\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"article-iac__wrapper\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe security tips provided earlier in this guide still apply: If you can protect yourself from getting hacked, you will have a better shot at preventing yourself from being surveilled (when it comes to surveilling iPhones, for instance governments often have few options \u003Ci\u003E besides\u003C\u002Fi\u003E hacking them.) \u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EGovernments have a weapon in their hands that criminal hackers do not: the power of the law. Many of the tips in this section of the guide will help you not only against legal requests and government hacking, but also against anyone else who may be trying to spy on you, \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fmotherboard.vice.com\u002Fen_us\u002Ftopic\u002Fwhen-spies-come-home\" target=\"_blank\"\u003Esuch as an abusive partner\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EYou don’t have to turn yourself into a security expert. Just start thinking about your risks, and don’t be intimidated by the technology. Security is an ongoing process of learning. Both the threats and the tools developed to address them are constantly changing, which is one of the reasons why privacy and security advice can often seem fickle and contradictory. But the tips below are a good starting point.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Ch2\u003E THREAT MODELING (privacy and surveillance edition)\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E \u003Cp\u003EKeep in mind that different tools address different problems. Without threat modelling, it’s easy to feel overwhelmed by how many tools are out there. Threat modeling for surveillance is similar to threat modelling for hacking, but there are of course some nuances that vary in every situation.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EIt’s easy for some people to say “use Signal, use Tor,” and be done with it, but that doesn’t work for everyone. For example, a friend used to message people about her abusive ex-partner using the built-in \u003Ci\u003E Words With Friends\u003C\u002Fi\u003E messenger, because she knew that he read her text messages and Gchats. \u003Ci\u003E Words With Friends\u003C\u002Fi\u003E does not have a particularly secure messaging system, but in this case it was a better option than Signal or Hangouts because he didn’t think to read her messages on the game.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"ad-unit__container__container\"\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"article-iac__wrapper\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cp\u003EWhen it comes to state actors, it might be helpful to think of surveillance in two different forms: surveillance of metadata (who you are, who you’re talking to, when you’re talking) and surveillance of content (what you are saying.) As with all things, when you dig a little deeper, it’s not as simple as that. But if you’re thinking about this for the first time, it’s a good start.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003ESurveillance law is complicated, but long story short, both the law and current technological infrastructure in the United States make it easier to grab metadata than content. Metadata isn’t necessarily \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.eff.org\u002Fdeeplinks\u002F2013\u002F06\u002Fwhy-metadata-matters\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E less important or revealing\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E than content. Say Planned Parenthood called you. Then you call your partner. Then you call your insurance. Then you call the abortion clinic. That information is going to be on your phone bill, and your telephone provider may easily give it up to the government. Your cell provider might not be recording those calls—the content is still private. But at that point, the content doesn’t really matter—it would be easy for someone with the metadata alone to have a reasonable idea of what your calls were about.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EStart thinking about what is open and exposed, and what you can protect. Sometimes, you have to accept that there’s very little you can do about a particular channel of communication. If circumstances are dire, you’re going to just have to work around it.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Ch2\u003E SIGNAL\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E \u003Cp\u003ESignal is an encrypted messaging service for smartphones and desktop computers. It is, for many—but not all—people, a good option for avoiding surveillance. Because the government has the capability to intercept electronic messages while they’re being transmitted, you want to use end-to-end encryption for as many of your communications as possible.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"ad-unit__container__container\"\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"article-iac__wrapper\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cp\u003EUsing Signal is easy. You can find it and install it from your phone’s app store. (In the iOS App Store and the Google Play Store, it’s called “Signal Private Messenger,” and it’s made by \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwhispersystems.org\u002F\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E Open Whisper Systems\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E.)\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EIf you have the other person’s phone number in your contacts list, you can see them in Signal, and message them or call them. As long as the other person also has Signal, the messages automatically encrypt—all the work is invisible.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EIt even has a desktop app, so you can use it the way that iOS\u002FMac OS people use iMessage on both their phones and computers. Go to the Signal.org website and download the app for your preferred operating system. Just follow the instructions—trust us, they’re easy (Note: the desktop app has had a \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fmotherboard.vice.com\u002Fen_us\u002Farticle\u002Fbj3pxd\u002Fsignal-disappearing-messages-not-disappearing\" target=\"_blank\"\u003Efew\u003C\u002Fa\u003E \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fmotherboard.vice.com\u002Fen_us\u002Farticle\u002Fkzke7z\u002Fsignal-disappearing-messages-are-stored-indefinitely-on-mac-hard-drives\" target=\"_blank\"\u003Ebugs\u003C\u002Fa\u003E and, very generally, computers are less safe than smartphones so if you’re an at-risk person, perhaps stick to the app.) \u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003ESignal also lets you set a timer for messages to automatically expire, thus deleting them from all devices. You can set the timer for all kinds of lengths, including very short ones. This is a great feature for journalists who are concerned about protecting their sources or their conversations with editors. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EDisappearing messages, ease-of-use, and end-to-end encryption are great features, and they’re part of the reason why we recommend Signal over many other end-to-end messaging apps for your most sensitive communications. iMessage and WhatsApp also use end-to-end encryption, but they both have drawbacks.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"ad-unit__container__container\"\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"article-iac__wrapper\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cp\u003EWe do not recommend WhatsApp, because WhatsApp is owned by Facebook, and \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.theverge.com\u002F2016\u002F8\u002F25\u002F12638698\u002Fwhatsapp-to-start-sharing-user-data-with-facebook\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E has been sharing user information with its parent company\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E. While this is only metadata, it is ultimately a rollback of a privacy promise made when WhatsApp was acquired by Facebook. We think this says something negative about the overall trustworthiness of the company in coming days—and there are disturbing rumors about \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Farstechnica.com\u002Finformation-technology\u002F2018\u002F04\u002Fhours-after-data-clash-report-whatsapp-founder-says-hes-leaving-facebook\u002F\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EFacebook considering weakening its encryption\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EIt is a very good thing that Apple encrypts iMessages end-to-end. But \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fsupport.apple.com\u002Fkb\u002FPH12519?locale=en_US\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E iMessage also backs up messages to iCloud\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E by default, which is why you can message from all your Apple devices. This is a great and fun feature, but if you’re concerned about government surveillance, remember that Apple complies with lawful government demands for data in your iCloud: “iMessage and SMS messages are backed up on iCloud for your convenience,” Apple’s privacy page states. You can turn this feature off, but in theory Apple could be forced to access the iMessages you’ve sent people who still have the feature enabled.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003ESignal keeps very little information. We know this, because Open Whisper Systems was subpoenaed by the government last year, and was forced to hand over information. But the information it had—by design—was pretty minimal. \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Farstechnica.com\u002Ftech-policy\u002F2016\u002F10\u002Ffbi-demands-signal-user-data-but-theres-not-much-to-hand-over\u002F\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E Signal retains phone number, account creation date, and the time of the user’s last connection to Signal servers\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E. Yes, that’s still something, but as you can see, it’s not very much.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"ad-unit__container__container\"\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"article-iac__wrapper\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThere are worse products to use than iMessage and WhatsApp. For example, \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fmotherboard.vice.com\u002Fread\u002Fencryption-app-telegram-probably-isnt-as-secure-for-terrorists-as-isis-thinks\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E you absolutely should avoid using Telegram\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E for sensitive communications. And Google can read your Hangout chats unless you take additional steps to encrypt them end-to-end. There are several other products on the market that are decent alternatives (for example, \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fplay.google.com\u002Fstore\u002Fapps\u002Fdetails?id=com.wire&amp;hl=en\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E Wire\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E or \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwickr.com\u002Fproducts\u002Fpersonal\u002F\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EWickr\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.) One thing that’s worth mentioning about Signal is that it requires you to associate the device with a phone number. This means that you need to trust the people you’re messaging to have your phone number ( \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fmotherboard.vice.com\u002Fen_us\u002Farticle\u002F9kaxge\u002Fhow-to-use-signal-without-giving-out-your-phone-number-a-gendered-security-issue\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E or need to jump through hoops to use Signal with a dummy phone number\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E); there are many reasons why you might want to message people without giving them your phone number, which is one of the potential drawbacks of Signal. If this is a concern for you, consider another option.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EAnother thing to remember is that just because a communication is end-to-end encrypted doesn’t mean it’s invisible to the government. It just means the contents are encrypted \u003Ci\u003E between\u003C\u002Fi\u003E endpoints. You can see the message, your recipient can see the message. If it’s intercepted in transit, it’s completely garbled, and the content of your message is protected from spying eyes.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EBut if an “endpoint” is compromised—in other words, if your own phone is hacked or physically seized by the government, or your texting partner is screencapping your conversation—it’s game over. This is why it’s good to turn on disappearing messages. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"ad-unit__container__container\"\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"article-iac__wrapper\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cp\u003EEncryption doesn’t make it impossible for the government to snoop, it just makes it way more challenging. The point is that introducing friction into the equation does provide privacy.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Ch2\u003E SOCIAL MEDIA\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E \u003Cp\u003EIf you post publicly on social media, know that local police (and likely federal agencies as well) keep tabs on activists online. For example, Facebook, Instagram, and Twitter have all fed data to social media monitoring products that \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.aclunc.org\u002Fblog\u002Ffacebook-instagram-and-twitter-provided-data-access-surveillance-product-marketed-target\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E police departments used to track Black Lives Matter activists\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EEven if you keep your privacy settings on lockdown, social media companies are subject to subpoenas, court orders, and data requests for your information. And sometimes, they’ll fork over the information without ever notifying the user that it’s happening. For the purposes of social media, assume that everything you post is public. This doesn’t mean you should stop using social media, it just means you have to be mindful of how you use it.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EIf you’re an activist, consider using a pseudonym for your activism. If you post online at all, take others’ safety and privacy into consideration as well.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EWho are you tagging into your posts? Are you adding location information? Who are you taking a picture of, and why? Be particularly careful with photos or posts about protests, rallies, or meetings. \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fmotherboard.vice.com\u002Fread\u002Fthat-time-the-super-bowl-secretly-used-facial-recognition-software-on-fans\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E Facial recognition technology\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E is fairly sophisticated—\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fmotherboard.vice.com\u002Fen_us\u002Farticle\u002F3k4dq5\u002Famazon-rekognition-facial-recognition-cops-body-cameras\" target=\"_blank\"\u003Eand cheap\u003C\u002Fa\u003E—now, so even if you leave people untagged, theoretically an algorithm could scan for and identify activists in a photograph of a rally. You can already see this at work in Facebook’s tag suggestions.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"ad-unit__container__container\"\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"article-iac__wrapper\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cp\u003EWhen you take a picture of someone at a protest, make sure that they consent, and that they know the implications of having a photo of themselves out there.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Ch2\u003E DEVICE CAMERAS, MICROPHONES, AND THE INTERNET OF THINGS\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E \u003Cp\u003EDo you live around any cameras? If you use internet-connected security cameras inside your home, or have a webcam running, don’t leave these things unsecured. Make sure that you’ve changed any passwords from the default that they shipped with, and cover them when you’re not using them.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EIf you have a laptop or a smartphone, use a sticker to cover the front-facing camera. You don’t have to stop FaceTiming and taking selfies, you just want to cover things up so no one’s looking at you when you don’t want them to. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EPutting an Amazon Alexa, Google Home, Facebook Portal, or a smart TV in your house can potentially be risky because those devices can be used to record audio and sometimes video, and, at the very least, can \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fsupport.google.com\u002Fgooglehome\u002Fanswer\u002F7072285?hl=en-GB\" target=\"_blank\"\u003Ebe used to target ads at you\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. Even if those companies aren’t using them to spy on you, data they record can be subject to a warrant or subpoena. Likewise, you probably don’t want to load your home with internet of things-connected devices and smart appliances, many of which are insecure and most of which have terrible privacy policies.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EFinally, there is absolutely no way to make sure your microphone is not recording. If you’re concerned about being wiretapped, consider turning off your phone and putting it in the microwave ( \u003Cb\u003Etemporarily, with the microwave off\u003C\u002Fb\u003E), or leaving your phone in the other room. Turning your phone off alone \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.schneier.com\u002Fblog\u002Farchives\u002F2006\u002F12\u002Fremotely_eavesd_1.html\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E does not necessarily protect you\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E! And consider leaving all your devices outside of the bedroom when you have sex with your partner.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"ad-unit__container__container\"\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"article-iac__wrapper\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIn 2012, Khadija Ismayilova, an Azeri journalist, was \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.independent.co.uk\u002Fnews\u002Fworld\u002Feurope\u002Fsex-video-used-to-blackmail-azerbaijani-journalist-7575834.html\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E blackmailed with a surreptitiously filmed sex tape\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E. The blackmailer told Ismayilova to stop publishing articles critical of the government, or else have her tape released. (Ismayilova went public, and the tape was posted on the internet.) In 2015, the Azerbaijan government \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.theguardian.com\u002Fworld\u002F2015\u002Fsep\u002F01\u002Fazerbaijan-khadija-ismayilova-verdict\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E sentenced her to seven and a half years in prison\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E on tax evasion charges. She is \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.aljazeera.com\u002Fnews\u002F2016\u002F05\u002Fazerbaijan-releases-khadija-ismayilova-jail-160525144400012.html\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E currently out on probation\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EGovernments at home and abroad have used sex to blackmail dissenters. Be aware of that, and protect your privacy.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Ch2\u003E LOCK SCREEN\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E \u003Cp\u003EPut a password\u002Fpasscode on your phone and your computer. Don’t rely on your thumbprint alone. The police are more likely to be able to legally compel you to use your \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.theatlantic.com\u002Ftechnology\u002Farchive\u002F2016\u002F05\u002Fiphone-fingerprint-search-warrant\u002F480861\u002F\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E fingerprint to open up your phone\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E. You may have a stronger constitutional right \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.eff.org\u002Fissues\u002Fknow-your-rights#17\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E not to speak your password\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Ch2\u003E USE OTR FOR CHATTING (if you have to)\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E \u003Cp\u003EIt’s best to use Signal when chatting with people. But here’s another option that’s particularly useful for journalists.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EClose your Gmail window and use OTR (Off The Record) instead to chat. Keep in mind that you can only use OTR if the other person is also using OTR. Mac users can \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fssd.eff.org\u002Fen\u002Fmodule\u002Fhow-use-otr-mac\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E install Adium\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E, PC (and Linux) users will have to \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fssd.eff.org\u002Fen\u002Fmodule\u002Fhow-use-otr-windows\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E install Pidgin and the OTR plugin\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EYou can use your Gmail account as your chat ID. So what’s going on is that you’re engaging in Gchat, but with a layer of encryption on top. Open up a chat window and click the lock icon to begin encryption. And make sure you tweak your settings so that you’re not retaining chat logs during encrypted conversations.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"ad-unit__container__container\"\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"article-iac__wrapper\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAgain, end-to-end only goes so far. \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.wired.com\u002F2011\u002F07\u002Fmanning-lamo-logs\u002F\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E If the other person is logging your conversations\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E, it might not matter that you went this far. If you’re concerned, ask your friend to stop logging.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Ch2\u003E THE TOR BROWSER\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E \u003Cp\u003ETor—which originally took its name from an acronym for “The Onion Router”—scrambles your internet traffic by routing it through several layers of computers. This way, when you access a website, it can’t tell where you’re connecting from. The easiest way to use Tor is just to install the \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.torproject.org\u002Fprojects\u002Ftorbrowser.html.en\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E Tor Browser\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E. It’s just like Firefox or Chrome or Microsoft Edge, the connection is just a lot slower because of the privacy it provides.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EUsing Tor for everything will give you a big privacy boost, but it’s a bit unwieldy. Don’t, for instance, try to stream Netflix over Tor.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EEvaluate your needs and figure out how much Tor you need in your life. Always remember that your IP address (which can give away where you are, and therefore, who you might be) is laid bare if you aren’t using it.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EThere are four reasons why you might want to use Tor.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cul\u003E \u003Cli\u003EYou’re trying to keep your identity hidden.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E \u003Cli\u003EYou use a lot of public WiFi.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E \u003Cli\u003EYou’re trying to get around government censorship.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E \u003Cli\u003EYou are protecting the other people who use Tor.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E \u003C\u002Ful\u003E \u003Cp\u003EIf you’re an activist who is trying to hide their identity, you need Tor to mask your IP address. This is a limited use case scenario. For example, it’s self-defeating for me to open up Tor, log into my public Twitter account, and tweet, “What up, everyone, I’m tweeting from the Vice Media offices in New York City.” I am giving away all the information that Tor is masking for me—because when it comes down to it, in that use case scenario, I was never planning on keeping it private.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"ad-unit__container__container\"\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"article-iac__wrapper\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIf you connect to a lot of public Wi-Fi (think Starbucks, a hotel, or the airport), though, you should use Tor. It provides similar benefits as VPNs. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EIf your government begins to censor parts of the web, \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fmotherboard.vice.com\u002Fread\u002Fturks-are-flocking-to-tor-after-government-orders-block-of-anti-censorship-tools\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E as many governments do\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E, Tor might be able to help you get around that. Tor certainly helps people connecting to the internet from other countries that practice internet censorship.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EFinally, the thing about Tor is that the more people use it, the less trackable everyone else is. When a lot of random, unaffiliated people from all over the world use it, it becomes stronger and stronger. If you take the time to use Tor every day, you are helping people who really do need it.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EA couple caveats, here: Tor is not bulletproof. The government has been known to monitor groups of users on Tor, \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Farstechnica.com\u002Fsecurity\u002F2015\u002F10\u002Fhow-the-nsa-can-break-trillions-of-encrypted-web-and-vpn-connections\u002F\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E just like it’s been known to hack VPN users en masse\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E. Tor has been criticized for its security flaws and because until recently it lacked a sandbox, which is a security feature that limits apps from reaching data outside of those apps. Tor is for privacy, not security. And Tor is designed to make it \u003Ci\u003E hard \u003C\u002Fi\u003Eto unmask your activity, not \u003Ci\u003E impossible\u003C\u002Fi\u003E, so there’s always a risk that you aren’t being hidden.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EThe computers that make up the Tor network—the ones that your traffic bounces through—are run by volunteers, institutions, and organizations all over the world, \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fmotherboard.vice.com\u002Fread\u002Fthe-operators\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E some of whom face legal risks for doing so\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E. They are not supposed to log the traffic that goes through them, but because it’s a volunteer network, some might. The risk is mitigated by the fact that each node only sees a snapshot of the traffic running through it, and nobody has access to both the user’s original IP and their unencrypted traffic. A bad actor would have to run a very large number of Tor nodes to start logging meaningful traffic—which would be difficult—and the Tor project monitors for behavior that suggests anybody might be doing that. Tor was not designed to withstand a global, passive adversary—such as an intelligence agency—sweeping up very large mountains of traffic.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"ad-unit__container__container\"\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"article-iac__wrapper\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cp\u003EUltimately, for the purposes of state surveillance, Tor is better than a VPN, and a VPN is better than nothing.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Ch2\u003E VIRTUAL PRIVATE NETWORKS\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E \u003Cp\u003EWhen it comes to high level state surveillance, VPNs may not help much. A VPN will obscure your IP address, but VPNs can be subpoenaed for user information that may ultimately identify you. For example, \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Farstechnica.com\u002Fsecurity\u002F2016\u002F06\u002Faiming-for-anonymity-ars-assesses-the-state-of-vpns-in-2016\u002F\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E many VPN companies keep logs\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E on what IP addresses log on when and what sites are accessed—which can end up pinpointing you, especially if you used your credit card to pay for a VPN subscription.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003ESome VPN companies claim not to log user information. \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fmotherboard.vice.com\u002Fen_us\u002Farticle\u002Fvvkzbd\u002Fthe-best-vpns-ranked\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EYou need to evaluate how much you trust these companies\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, and make that decision for yourself. If what you’re concerned about is government surveillance, our recommendation is that you stick with Tor. You could also run your own VPN server and deliberately decide not to log any of the traffic, although that may require some more technical knowledge.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Ch2\u003E PGP (probably isn’t worth the trouble)\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E \u003Cp\u003EA relatively common way to encrypt your email is with a program called PGP, or Pretty Good Privacy. However, PGP is incredibly obnoxious to use. \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fmotherboard.vice.com\u002Fread\u002Feven-the-inventor-of-pgp-doesnt-use-pgp\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E Even PGP’s creator Phil Zimmermann has stopped using it, since he can’t use it on his phone\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E. The problem isn’t just that \u003Ci\u003E you \u003C\u002Fi\u003Ehave to figure out PGP, everyone you talk to also has to figure it out. Telling someone to download Signal or Wire is a lot easier than walking them through public\u002Fprivate key encryption. This is where your threat model comes in handy, to help figure out if PGP is actually worth it to you.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"ad-unit__container__container\"\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"article-iac__wrapper\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIf you absolutely must use encrypted email, \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Femailselfdefense.fsf.org\u002Fen\u002F\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E this guide to PGP might be helpful\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E. It’s tricky, so you might want to go to a crypto party and have an activist or technologist help you set it up. You could also use one of the email providers that automatically encrypts emails between accounts on its service, such as ProtonMail or Tutanota.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Ch2\u003E PRIVATE EMAIL SERVERS (don't do it)\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E \u003Cp\u003EIf 2016 and Hillary Clinton’s presidential bid did anything, it convinced everyone \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fmotherboard.vice.com\u002Fread\u002Fhillary-clintons-no-good-very-bad-email-security\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E not to run their own private email server\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EIt’s true that Google and other companies have to comply with court orders for your information, including your emails. But on the other hand, Google knows how to run email servers way better than you do. Email servers are hard!\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EIf you are encrypting email, Google can only practically hand over the metadata (who’s sending to whom and subject headers). Since encrypting email is a huge pain, try to keep all your sensitive stuff away from email, and in easier to secure channels instead. Don’t abandon your third-party email account, just be aware that the government can get at what’s inside.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Ch2\u003E ENCRYPT YOUR HARD DRIVE\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E \u003Cp\u003EGood news: this isn’t as hard as it used to be!\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EFull-disk encryption means that once your device is locked , the contents of your hard drive can’t be accessed without your password\u002Fkey.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EA lot of smartphones come with full disk encryption built in. If you own an iPhone with a recently updated operating system (like, in the last four years, really), just slap a passcode on that sucker and you’re golden.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"ad-unit__container__container\"\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"article-iac__wrapper\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIf you own an Android phone, it might already be encrypted by default (Google Pixel is). But chances are, it’s not. There isn’t an up-to-date guide on turning on encryption on all Android devices, so you’re going to have to poke around yourself, or ask a friend. As for computers, things are again, much easier than they used to be. Use your operating system’s full disk encryption option instead. For MacBooks running OS X Lion or newer, just \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fsupport.apple.com\u002Fen-us\u002FHT204837\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E turn on FileVault\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E. (But you should really be running the most up to date Mac operating system anyway.)\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EWith Windows, on the other hand, \u003Ci\u003E some \u003C\u002Fi\u003Eusers have encryption by default, such as Windows 10 Pro users. Some more users can turn it on, \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Farstechnica.com\u002Fgadgets\u002F2015\u002F08\u002Fphone-and-laptop-encryption-guide-protect-your-stuff-and-yourself\u002F2\u002F\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E but it’s kind of a pain\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E. And if you’re using Microsoft’s Bitlocker, you’re going to have to fiddle with \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Farstechnica.com\u002Finformation-technology\u002F2015\u002F12\u002Fmicrosoft-may-have-your-encryption-key-heres-how-to-take-it-back\u002F\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E some additional settings to make it more secure\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E (it’s worth noting that flaws in SSDs recently allowed \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.zdnet.com\u002Farticle\u002Fflaws-in-self-encrypting-ssds-let-attackers-bypass-disk-encryption\u002F\" target=\"_blank\"\u003Eresearchers to bypass Bitlocker’s encryption\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.) Apple doesn’t retain the capability of unlocking your devices. Famously, if the government goes to Apple, Apple can’t just decrypt your phone for the feds, \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fmotherboard.vice.com\u002Ftag\u002FApple+vs+FBI\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E not without coming up with a hack that will affect every iPhone in the world\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E. But Microsoft isn’t doing quite the same thing—in some cases they use what’s known as “key escrow,” meaning they can decrypt your machine—so you have to take additional steps ( \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Farstechnica.com\u002Finformation-technology\u002F2015\u002F12\u002Fmicrosoft-may-have-your-encryption-key-heres-how-to-take-it-back\u002F\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003Eoutlined in this article\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E) to get that same level of protection.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"ad-unit__container__container\"\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"article-iac__wrapper\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cp\u003EYou may need to resort to using \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fveracrypt.codeplex.com\u002F\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E VeraCrypt\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E, which isn’t full disk encryption but can create an encrypted part of your hard-drive. A lot of older guides will say to use TrueCrypt, regardless of operating system. This is now outdated advice. VeraCrypt used to be TrueCrypt, and the story of why it's not any more is a convoluted crypto soap opera with plot holes the size of Mars, and it is frankly outside the scope of this guide. Long story short, there’s nothing wrong with VeraCrypt as far as the experts can tell, but if you have the option, use the full disk encryption that your operating system already provided.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EIf you use Linux, your distro probably supports encryption out of the box. Follow the instructions while installing.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Ch2\u003E CREDIT CARDS\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E \u003Cp\u003EKnow that credit card companies never stand up to the government. If you pay for anything using your credit card, know that the government can get that information pretty easily. And remember that once your identity touches something, there’s a chain that the government can follow all the way back.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EFor example, if you get a prepaid Visa gift card using your personal credit card, and pay a VPN company with that, the government can just go backwards through the chain and find your personal credit card, and then you. If you pay a VPN company with Bitcoin, but you bought the Bitcoin through a Bitcoin exchange using your personal credit card, that’s traceable as well.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"ad-unit__container__container\"\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"article-iac__wrapper\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThis applies to anything else you use money for, like buying domains or cheap, pay-as-you-go phones, known as burners. Practically speaking, there’s not a lot you can do about this. It’s one of the reasons why we recommend Tor instead of a VPN service for certain situations.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EIt’s also one of the reasons why it’s so hard to get a burner phone that’s \u003Ci\u003E really \u003C\u002Fi\u003Ea burner. There is no easy answer here. We’re not going to pretend to be able to give good advice in this instance. If you find yourself in a situation where your life depends on staying anonymous, you’re going to need a lot more help than any internet guide.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EOne more thing: For now, organizations like the ACLU and NAACP have a \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fen.wikipedia.org\u002Fwiki\u002FNAACP_v._Alabama\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E constitutional right to resist giving up the names of donors\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E. But your credit card or PayPal might betray you anyways. This doesn’t mean you \u003Ci\u003E shouldn’t\u003C\u002Fi\u003E donate to organizations that resist oppression and fight for civil rights and civil liberties. Rather, it makes it all the more important that you do. The more ordinary people do so, the more that individual donors are protected from scrutiny and suspicion.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Ch2\u003E DATA RETENTION FOR LAWYERS, JOURNALISTS, AND ANYONE ELSE\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E \u003Cp\u003EWant to protect your sources? Your clients? Your notes, your Slack chats, your Gchats, your Google Drive, your Dropbox, your recorded interviews, your transcripts, and your texts can all end up in court. Depending on what kind of court case it is, it might not matter that it’s encrypted.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EDon’t wait until a lawsuit is imminent to delete all your stuff. That might be illegal, and you might be risking going to jail. Every situation is different: your notes might be necessary to get you out of trouble. So if you’re the type to hoard notes, know the risk, talk to a lawyer, and act responsibly.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"ad-unit__container__container\"\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"article-iac__wrapper\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Ch2\u003E LOG OFF\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E \u003Cp\u003EMany public places have cameras, some spots are \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.theguardian.com\u002Flaw\u002F2015\u002Fjul\u002F17\u002Fshotspotter-gunshot-detection-schools-campuses-privacy\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E \u003Cb\u003E wired with microphones\u003C\u002Fb\u003E \u003C\u002Fa\u003E. And there’s always the possibility that you are being individually targeted for surveillance. But ultimately, it’s a lot harder to surveil someone in person than to collect the electronic communications of many people at the same time.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003ETake a break from the wired world and meet people in person. If you stay out of earshot, you won’t be overheard, and your words will melt into the air, unsurveilled and unrecorded.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EAnd besides, if you’re reading this guide, chances are that you really need a hug right now.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003ESo meet up with your friends, verify your Signal keys, and give each other a big hug. Because you’re probably both scared, and you need each other more than you need any of this technology.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Ch2\u003E GO OUT THERE AND BE SAFE\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E \u003Cp\u003EThat is all for now. Again, this is just meant to be a basic guide for average computer users. So if you're a human rights activist working in a dangerous country or a war zone, or an organization building IT infrastructure on the fly, this is certainly not enough, and you may need more precautions.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EBut these are essential tips that everyone should know about.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003EOf course, some readers will leap at the chance to point out everything that may have been missing from this guide, and we'd like to hear your feedback. Security is a constantly changing world, and what's good advice today might not be good advice tomorrow, so our goal is to keep this guide updated somewhat regularly, so, please, do reach out (email \u003Ca href=\"mailto:editors@motherboard.tv\" target=\"_blank\"\u003Eeditors@motherboard.tv\u003C\u002Fa\u003E and put the title of this guide in the subject please) if you think we have something wrong or missing something.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cdiv class=\"ad-unit__container__container\"\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"article-iac__wrapper\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAnd remember, always be vigilant!\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003E VERSION HISTORY\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fmotherboard.vice.com\u002Fen_us\u002Farticle\u002Fbmv5a4\u002Fthe-motherboard-guide-to-not-getting-hacked\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EVersion 1.0\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, August 2, 2016.\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fassets.documentcloud.org\u002Fdocuments\u002F4222455\u002FThe-Motherboard-Guide-To-Not-Getting-Hacked.pdf\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EVersion 2.0\u003C\u002Fa\u003E [PDF], November 15, 2017. To verify the integrity of the PDF, this is the original file's SHA hash: 2938d3b9b9842ae0763ab0dd27696de3dce0dd96. Weakest Link","id":"57a20870a0bae4df0c021052","slug":"the-weakest-link","__typename":"Topic"},"Topic:58010e3572df083eef56f8ca":{"name":"Infosec","id":"58010e3572df083eef56f8ca","slug":"infosec","__typename":"Topic"},"Topic:5886b6faaa0e2845abcf7460":{"name":"Motherboard long-form-v2--center m-t-4--xs m-b-6--xs"></div><p class="long-form-v2__credit long-form-v2--center ff--accent lh--headline size--6 uppercased">Image:&nbsp;Lia Kantrowitz/Jason Koebler</p><div class="article-keep-reading"><div class="article-keep-reading__children-container m-t-5--xs" tabindex="-1"><div data-type="body-text" class="article__body article__body dsp-block-xx ff--body-article size--article lh--body"><p><i> <b>Editors' note:</b> This is a comprehensive guide to digital security, which will be regularly updated and replaces some of our old guides, brought to you by Motherboard, a VICE site about all things tech and the future. There is a version history at the bottom of this post.</i></p> <p><i> <b>Last update</b>: November 12, 2018. This is <a href="https://pastebin.com/5XMmwhqB" target="_blank">also available as a plaintext file</a>.</i> </p> <hr> <p>One of the questions we are asked most often at Motherboard is “how can I prevent myself from getting hacked?”</p> <div class="ad-unit__container__container"><div class="article-iac__wrapper"></div></div><p>Because living in modern society necessitates putting an uncomfortably large amount of trust in third parties, the answer is often “not a whole lot.” We said that last year when we published this guide, and, since then, that reality has become even more obvious. Take, for example, <a href="https://motherboard.vice.com/en_us/article/d3qejz/facebook-hack-13-million-victims-search-history" target="_blank">the recent Facebook hack</a> that affected nearly 30 million users worldwide, exposing the location and search history of 14 million of them. The hackers behind the breach took advantage of a useful privacy feature and leveraged it to steal digital “tokens” that gave the hackers full access to victims’ accounts. There was nothing those users did wrong, nor was there anything they could’ve done to prevent getting hacked aside from not having a Facebook account at all.</p> <p>We live in a world where hackers steal <a href="https://motherboard.vice.com/read/427-million-myspace-passwords-emails-data-breach" target="_blank"> <b> hundreds of millions</b> </a> of <a href="https://motherboard.vice.com/read/another-day-another-hack-117-million-linkedin-emails-and-password" target="_blank"> <b> passwords</b> </a> from companies in one swoop and occasionally cause <a href="https://motherboard.vice.com/read/attack-on-ukraine-power-grid-came-from-russian-isp" target="_blank"> <b> large-scale blackouts</b> </a>. The future is probably not going to get better, with <a href="https://motherboard.vice.com/read/the-internet-of-things-will-cause-the-first-ever-large-scale-internet-disaster" target="_blank"> <b> real-life disasters caused by internet-connected knick-knacks</b> </a>, <a href="https://motherboard.vice.com/read/smart-robots-dhs-report" target="_blank"> <b> smart home robots that could kill you</b> </a>, and your telecom providers who routinely <a href="https://motherboard.vice.com/en_us/article/a3qpk5/t-mobile-hack-data-breach-api-customer-data" target="_blank">lose customer data</a> and unwittingly help hackers <a href="https://motherboard.vice.com/en_us/article/vbqax3/hackers-sim-swapping-steal-phone-numbers-instagram-bitcoin" target="_blank">steal your phone number</a> (and sometimes your money.) Meanwhile, an ever-growing and increasingly passive surveillance apparatus that has trickled down to state and local police is an ever-present threat to our digital privacy and increasingly uses technology that is <a href="https://motherboard.vice.com/en_us/article/3k4dq5/amazon-rekognition-facial-recognition-cops-body-cameras" target="_blank">developed by Silicon Valley giants</a> who are supposedly consumer-focused.</p> <div class="ad-unit__container__container"><div class="article-iac__wrapper"></div></div><p>That doesn’t mean it’s hopeless out there. There are lots of things you can do to make it much more difficult for hackers or would-be surveillers to access your devices and accounts, and the aim of this guide is to give you clear, easy-to-follow steps to improve your digital security. There are, broadly speaking, two types of hacks: Those that are unpreventable by users because of the trust we must place in third parties in order to live a normal life, and those you can generally prevent. We want to help you mitigate the damage of the first and prevent the second from happening.</p> <p>You, as an individual user, can’t do anything to prevent your email provider, your cell phone provider, or the company that holds your financial details from getting hacked. But you can avoid phishing attacks that will let a hacker get into your individual email account, and you can also prevent a password obtained in a larger hack from being reused on another, separate account you have.</p> <p>This guide isn’t comprehensive and it’s not personalized; there is no such thing as “perfect security” and there are no one-size-fits all solutions. Instead, we hope this will be a jumping-off point for people looking to batten down the hatches on their digital lives.</p><div style="max-width:100vh;" data-iframely-id="HpwmweL" data-embedded-url="https://www.acast.com/cyber/simhijackingandthephonenumberransom?autoplay=true" class="article__embed article__embed--iframely"><div style="left:0;width:100%;height:0;position:relative;padding-bottom:100%;" data-iframely-smart-iframe="true"><iframe data-img="" data-iframely-url="//oembed.vice.com/HpwmweL" style="border:0;top:0;left:0;width:100%;height:100%;position:absolute;" allowfullscreen="" data-iframely-smart-iframe="true" frameborder="0"></iframe></div></div><p><b> <i> CYBER is Motherboard’s new podcast about cybersecurity. </i></b><a href="https://itunes.apple.com/us/podcast/cyber/id1441708044?mt=2" target="_blank"><b> <i> Subscribe on Apple Podcasts</i></b></a><b> <i> or any podcast app.</i></b></p> <p>That’s why we’ve tried to keep this guide as accessible as possible, but if you run into any lingo you don’t know, there’s <a href="https://motherboard.vice.com/en_us/article/mg79v4/hacking-glossary" target="_blank">a glossary</a> to help out.</p> <div class="ad-unit__container__container"><div class="article-iac__wrapper"></div></div><p>The guide is the work of many people at Motherboard, both past and present. It has been vetted by several security experts, who we owe a great debt to. The tips and advice within it have grown out of years of writing and research on digital security by dozens of reporters and infosec professionals. Consider it a work-in-progress that will receive at least one big annual refresh, as well as smaller updates when major new vulnerabilities are exposed. Even if you read the guide last year, we think it’s worth it to read our new version, as lots of our advice has changed or become outdated as new threats have emerged and new tactics to keep yourself safe have become more accessible. </p> <p>And so, we present you the Motherboard Guide To Not Getting Hacked 3.0, with updates throughout the whole guide, and entire new sections. In the next few days, we will also publish several subject-specific guides about how to use the iPod as a secure communications device, how to securely wipe your computers and smartphones, and how to tell whether your accounts have been hacked. </p> <p>Stay safe, and stay vigilant.</p> <div class="article__media"> <picture class="article__image"> <source media="(max-width: 25em)" srcset="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAD/ACwAAAAAAQABAAACADs=" data-srcset="https://video-images.vice.com/_uncategorized/1542035959809-Screen-Shot-2018-11-12-at-95635-AM.jpeg?resize=1050:*, srcset="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAD/ACwAAAAAAQABAAACADs=" data-srcset="https://video-images.vice.com/_uncategorized/1542035959809-Screen-Shot-2018-11-12-at-95635-AM.jpeg?resize=1050:*, https://video-images.vice.com/_uncategorized/1542035959809-Screen-Shot-2018-11-12-at-95635-AM.jpeg?resize=1575:* 2x"> <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAD/ACwAAAAAAQABAAACADs=" alt="1542035959809-Screen-Shot-2018-11-12-at-95635-AM" class="col-12-xs" data-src="https://video-images.vice.com/_uncategorized/1542035959809-Screen-Shot-2018-11-12-at-95635-AM.jpeg"> </picture> <div class="article__image-caption"></div> </div> <h2>THREAT MODELING</h2> <p>Everything in this guide starts with “threat modeling,” which is hacker lingo for assessing how likely you are to get hacked or surveilled. When thinking about how to protect your digital communications, it is imperative that you first think about what you’re protecting and who you’re protecting it from. “Depends on your threat model” is a thing infosec pros say when asked questions about whether, say, Signal is the best messaging app or Tor is the best browser to use. The answer to any question about the “best” security decision is almost always “it depends.” </p> <div class="ad-unit__container__container"><div class="article-iac__wrapper"></div></div><p>No security plan is identical to any other. What sort of protections you take all depend on who may try to get into your accounts or read your messages. The bad news is that there are no silver bullets (sorry!), but the good news is that most people have threat models in which they probably don’t have to live like a paranoid recluse to be reasonably safe online.</p> <p>So before doing anything else, you should consider your threat model. Basically, what are you trying to protect, and who are you trying to protect it from?</p> <p>The Electronic Frontier Foundation recommends asking yourself these five questions when <a href="https://ssd.eff.org/en/module/introduction-threat-modeling" target="_blank">threat modeling</a>:</p> <ul> <li>What do you want to protect?</li> <li>Who do you want to protect it from?</li> <li>How likely is it that you will need to protect it?</li> <li>How bad are the consequences if you fail?</li> <li>How much trouble are you willing to go through in order to try to prevent those?</li> </ul> <p>Is your threat an ex who might want to go through your Facebook account? Then making sure they don't know your password is a good place to start. (Don't share critical passwords with people, no matter who they are; if we're talking Netflix or an account you create with the intention of sharing, make sure you never reuse that password elsewhere.) Are you trying to keep opportunistic people from pulling together your personal information—such as your birthday and the name of your high school—which in turn can be used to find other details? Well, keeping an eye on what sort of stuff you publish on social media would be a good idea. And two-factor authentication (more on that below) would go a long way to thwarting more serious criminals. If you are an activist, a journalist, or otherwise have reason to fear government, state, or law enforcement actors want to hack or surveil you, the steps you must take to protect yourself are significantly different than if you’re trying to keep plans for a surprise party secret from your best friend.</p> <div class="ad-unit__container__container"><div class="article-iac__wrapper"></div></div><p>Overestimating your threat can be a problem too: if you start using obscure custom operating systems, virtual machines, or anything else technical when it's really not necessary (or you don't know how to use it), you’re probably wasting your time and might be putting yourself at risk. At best, even the most simple tasks might take a while longer; in a worst-case scenario, you might be lulling yourself into a false sense of security with services and hardware that you don’t need, while overlooking what actually matters to you and the actual threats you might be facing.</p> <p>In certain places, this guide will offer specific steps to take if you have a threat model that includes sophisticated actors. But, in general, it’s designed for people who want to know the basics of how to strengthen their digital security. If your threat model includes NSA hackers or other state-sponsored groups like Fancy Bear, we recommend that you speak to a trained professional about your specific situation.</p> <h2> KEEP YOUR APPS UP TO DATE</h2> <p>Probably the most important and basic thing you can do to protect yourself is to update the software you use to its newest version. That means using an updated version of whatever operating system you're using, and updating all your apps. It also means updating the firmware on your router, connected devices, and any other gadgets you use that can connect to the internet.</p> <p>On your computer, you don't necessarily have to use the latest iteration of an operating system. In some cases, even slightly older versions of operating systems get security updates. (Unfortunately, this is no longer the case with Windows XP—<a href="http://mashable.com/2014/04/08/windows-xp-upgrade-or-switch/#qVE_iNROZGqP" target="_blank">stop using it</a>!) What's most important is that your OS is still receiving security updates, and that you're applying them.</p> <div class="ad-unit__container__container"><div class="article-iac__wrapper"></div></div><p>If you come away with one lesson from this guide, it should be update, update, update, or patch, patch, patch.</p> <p>Many common cyberattacks take advantage of flaws in outdated software such as old web browsers, PDF readers, or spreadsheet and word-processing tools. By keeping everything up to date, you have a much lower chance of becoming a victim of malware because responsible manufacturers and software developers quickly patch their products after new hacks are seen in the wild.</p> <p>Hacking is often a path of least resistance: you go after the easy, soft, targets first. For example, the hackers behind the destructive ransomware outbreak known as WannaCry hit victims who had not applied a security update that had been available for weeks. In other words, they knew they were going to get in because the victims had not changed the lock to their door even though their keys had already been made available to everyone.</p> <h2> PASSWORDS</h2> <p>We all have too many passwords to remember, which is why some people just reuse the same ones over and over. Reusing passwords is bad because if, for example, a hacker gets control of your Netflix or Spotify password, they can then use it to get into your ridesharing or online banking service to drain your bank account. Even though our brains <a href="https://motherboard.vice.com/read/your-brain-actually-doesnt-suck-at-passwords" target="_blank"> <b> aren't actually that bad at remembering passwords</b> </a>, it's almost impossible to remember dozens of unique, strong passwords.</p> <p>The good news is that the solution to these problems is already out there: password managers. These are apps or browser extensions that keep track of passwords for you, automatically help you create good passwords, and simplify your online life. If you use a manger, all you have to remember is one password, the one that unlocks the vault of your other passwords.</p> <div class="ad-unit__container__container"><div class="article-iac__wrapper"></div></div><p>That one password better be good though. Forget about capital letters, symbols, and numbers. The easiest way to make a secure master password is to make a passphrase: several random but pronounceable—and thus easier to memorize—words. For example: letdown birdie carbine mandrake alga gag (don’t use this one though, we just burned it.)</p> <p>Once you have that you can use unique passwords made of a lot of characters for everything else, as long as you create them with a password manager and never reuse them. The master password is better as a passphrase because it's easier to memorize, and the other passwords don't need to be memorized because the manager will remember them.</p> <p>Intuitively, you might think it's unwise to store your passwords on your computer or with a third-party password manager. What if a hacker gets in? Surely it's better that I'm keeping them all in my head? Well, not really: The risk of a crook reusing a shared password that has been stolen from somewhere else is far greater than some sophisticated hacker independently targeting your database of passwords. For example, if you used the same password across different websites, and that password was stolen in the massive Yahoo! hacks (which included 3 billion people), it could easily be reused on your Gmail, Uber, Facebook, and other accounts. Some password managers store your passwords encrypted in the cloud, so even if the company gets hacked, your passwords will be safe. For example, the password manager LastPass has been hacked at least twice, but no actual passwords were stolen because the company stored them securely. LastPass remains a recommended password manager despite those incidents. Again, it's all about understanding your own threat model.</p> <div class="ad-unit__container__container"><div class="article-iac__wrapper"></div></div><p>So, please, use <a href="http://lifehacker.com/5529133/five-best-password-managers" target="_blank"> <b> one of the many password managers</b> </a> out there, such as 1Password, LastPass, or KeePass. You can also probably use the password-storage feature included in modern browsers such as Chrome and Safari (but <a href="https://twitter.com/jtraceyjr/status/1045863115282362371?s=12" target="_blank">be careful</a> if you share your computer!) That’s better than not using a password manager at all, but a dedicated app is ideal. There's no reason not to do it. It will make you— <a href="https://motherboard.vice.com/read/your-shitty-password-hygiene-is-spreading-hacks-like-a-contagion-twitter-logins-hacked" target="_blank"> <b> and the rest of us</b> </a>—safer, and it'll even make your life easier because you won’t need to remember any passwords except your master password.</p> <p>If your employer asks you to change passwords periodically in the name of security, please tell them that's a terrible idea. <a href="https://arstechnica.com/information-technology/2016/08/frequent-password-changes-are-the-enemy-of-security-ftc-technologist-says/" target="_blank">Research shows</a> people are more likely to use weak, near identical passwords when forced to switch often. If you use a password manager, two-factor authentication (see the next section), and have unique strong passwords for every account there's no need to change them all the time—unless there’s a breach on the backend or your password is stolen somehow.</p> <h2> TWO-FACTOR AUTHENTICATION</h2> <p>Having unique, strong passwords is a great first step, but even those can be stolen. So you should add an extra layer of protection known as two-factor authentication (also known as two-step or 2FA) to your accounts. You should do this for any account that offers two-factor authentication, but you should <i> especially</i> make sure you do it on your most important ones (your email, your Facebook, Twitter accounts, your banking and financial accounts.) Again, a lot of services these days offer two-factor, so it doesn’t hurt to turn it on in as many places as you can. See all the services that offer 2FA at <a href="https://twofactorauth.org/" target="_blank"> <b> twofactorauth.org</b> </a>.</p> <div class="ad-unit__container__container"><div class="article-iac__wrapper"></div></div><p>By enabling two-factor you'll need something more than just your password to log into those accounts. Traditionally, this has been a numerical code sent to your cellphone via text message. But increasingly, the “second factor” is a code created by a specialized app, or a small, physical token like a USB key. Before talking about those options, however, we want to warn you that the specifics of using two-factor security have changed quite a bit since last year. </p> <p>Though you may be most familiar with using text messages(SMS) as a second factor, <a href="https://motherboard.vice.com/en_us/article/xyezmn/we-were-warned-about-flaws-in-the-mobile-data-backbone-for-years-now-2fa-is-screwed" target="_blank">this is no longer considered safe</a>. Thousands of people <a href="https://motherboard.vice.com/en_us/article/vbqax3/hackers-sim-swapping-steal-phone-numbers-instagram-bitcoin" target="_blank">have had their online accounts hacked</a> by relatively low-skilled criminals who were able to bypass two-factor authentication by stealing their phone number and intercepting their text message-based security codes. And the National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST), a part of the US government that writes guidelines on rules and measurements, including security, <a href="https://techcrunch.com/2016/07/25/nist-declares-the-age-of-sms-based-2-factor-authentication-over/" target="_blank"> <b> has discouraged</b> </a> the use of SMS-based 2FA.</p> <p>The recent attacks on thousands of people were made possible by “social engineering.” In the increasingly common attack known as “SIM swapping,” a customer service rep at a telecom company is tricked by a criminal into making a victim vulnerable. The attack involves getting phone companies to issue a new SIM card to the attackers, allowing them to take over the targeted phone number. That means when hackers use someone’s first factor (the password) to login to the target’s account, the second factor code is sent directly to the hacker, rather than the victim who owns the account. What’s worse, in some cases hackers don’t even need the password because the service they’re targeting allows to reset password using the phone number provided as second factor. This is an increasingly common hack.</p> <div class="ad-unit__container__container"><div class="article-iac__wrapper"></div></div><p>It's hard to defend against an attack like that, and it’s a sad truth that there is no form of perfect security. But there are steps you can take to make these attacks harder. </p> <p>What this means for two-factor authentication is that you should, if the website allows it, use another 2FA option that isn't SMS-based, such as an authentication app on your smartphone. Users familiar with SMS-based two-factor shouldn’t have much trouble making the switch. When you try to login to an account with two-factor enabled, you’ll still need to enter a six-digit code, but the code will be generated by the app on your phone, rather than being sent as a text message. Authentication apps are easy to use, with straightforward setup processes once you install them. Some good options are <a href="https://support.google.com/accounts/answer/1066447?hl=en" target="_blank"> <b> Google Authenticator</b> </a>, <a href="https://duo.com/product/trusted-users/two-factor-authentication/duo-mobile" target="_blank"> <b> DUO Mobile</b> </a>, and <a href="https://authy.com/" target="_blank"> <b> Authy</b> </a>. Besides offering better security than text message-based two factor, using these has the added advantage that they work offline, which means they work if your cellphone doesn't have coverage at the time you're logging in on another computer. This is especially great if you’re traveling internationally and logging into an account at, say, an internet cafe. Lots more sites now offer app-based 2FA, including Instagram and Twitter. </p> <p>The best solution, however, is a physical token or security key such as a <a href="https://www.yubico.com/products/yubikey-hardware/" target="_blank">YubiKey</a> or a <a href="https://cloud.google.com/titan-security-key/" target="_blank">Titan Security Key</a>, which connect to a computer via USB or wirelessly. You can set up these keys as the second factor for many services (here’s a <a href="https://support.google.com/accounts/answer/6103523" target="_blank">Google help page</a> on it.) Then when you login you will have to provide your password and insert the token into your computer and press a small button on the key itself to login. </p> <div class="ad-unit__container__container"><div class="article-iac__wrapper"></div></div><p>Security experts suggest using these keys because it’s virtually impossible for hackers to hack you using phishing attacks if you use them. Some hackers have been able to phish second factor codes by creating fake login pages. That doesn’t work if you use security keys. It’s important to remember, however, that you should be prepared in case you lose your security key. Buy and setup a backup key, setup the authenticator app, or <a href="https://motherboard.vice.com/en_us/article/evknva/what-is-a-two-factor-authentication-recovery-code" target="_blank">store recovery codes in a safe place</a>.</p> <div class="article__media"> <picture class="article__image"> <source media="(max-width: 25em)" srcset="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAD/ACwAAAAAAQABAAACADs=" srcset="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAAD/ACwAAAAAAQABAAACADs=" data-srcset="https://video-images.vice.com/_uncategorized/1542036024501-Screen-Shot-2018-11-12-at-95644-AM.jpeg?resize=850:*,
